        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/jacs.8b04183"><meta property="og:title" content="Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/jacs.8b04183.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Na-ion batteries are promising alternatives to Li-ion systems for electrochemical energy storage because of the higher natural abundance and widespread distribution of Na compared to Li. High capacity anode materials, such as phosphorus, have been explored to realize Na-ion battery technologies that offer comparable performances to their Li-ion counterparts. While P anodes provide unparalleled capacities, the mechanism of sodiation and desodiation is not well-understood, limiting further optimization. Here, we use a combined experimental and theoretical approach to provide molecular-level insight into the (de)sodiation pathways in black P anodes for sodium-ion batteries. A determination of the P binding in these materials was achieved by comparing to structure models created via species swapping, ab initio random structure searching, and a genetic algorithm. During sodiation, analysis of 31P chemical shift anisotropies in NMR data reveals P helices and P at the end of chains as the primary structural components in amorphous NaxP phases. X-ray diffraction data in conjunction with variable field 23Na magic-angle spinning NMR support the formation of a new Na3P crystal structure (predicted using density-functional theory) on sodiation. During desodiation, P helices are re-formed in the amorphous intermediates, albeit with increased disorder, yet emphasizing the pervasive nature of this motif. The pristine material is not re-formed at the end of desodiation and may be linked to the irreversibility observed in the Na–P system."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/jacs.8b04183.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6f0279cee775c-LHR"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6f0279cee775c-LHR">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '84b6fae1-5fb5-4d79-b71c-1cd9567328a6'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6f0279cee775c-LHR">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'JACS';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/JACS/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/JACS/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90f6f0279cee775c-LHR">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "jacsat";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.8b04183", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.8b04183", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.8b04183", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.8b04183", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.8b04183", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.8b04183", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.8b04183", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.8b04183", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.8b04183", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Morris</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">School of Metallurgy and Materials, University of Birmingham, Edgbaston, Birmingham B15 2TT, United Kingdom</div></div><span class="conrtib-corresp"><strong>*</strong><a href="mailto:a.j.morris.1@bham.ac.uk">a.j.morris.1@bham.ac.uk</a></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Andrew+J.++Morris" data-id="article_author_search">Andrew J. Morris</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" src="/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/orchid-2856f829046fbda55b90e1582edf0e9a.png" alt="Orcid"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-7453-5698" title="Orcid link">http://orcid.org/0000-0001-7453-5698</a></div></div></div></span></li><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Clare P. Grey</span><strong>*</strong><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Clare P. Grey</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">Department of Chemistry, University of Cambridge, Lensfield Road, Cambridge CB2 1EW, United Kingdom</div></div><span class="conrtib-corresp"><strong>*</strong><a href="mailto:cpg27@cam.ac.uk">cpg27@cam.ac.uk</a></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Clare+P.++Grey" data-id="article_author_search">Clare P. Grey</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" src="/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/orchid-2856f829046fbda55b90e1582edf0e9a.png" alt="Orcid"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-5572-192X" title="Orcid link">http://orcid.org/0000-0001-5572-192X</a></div></div></div></span></li></ul><div class="article_header-footer"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para"><span class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</span>. This publication is licensed under
                            <a href="https://pubs.acs.org/page/policy/authorchoice_ccby_termsofuse.html" class="link--bold" data-id="article_drawer_CC-License">CC-BY</a>.
                        </p></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/medium/ja-2018-04183b_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.8b04183&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Na-ion batteries are promising alternatives to Li-ion systems for electrochemical energy storage because of the higher natural abundance and widespread distribution of Na compared to Li. High capacity anode materials, such as phosphorus, have been explored to realize Na-ion battery technologies that offer comparable performances to their Li-ion counterparts. While P anodes provide unparalleled capacities, the mechanism of sodiation and desodiation is not well-understood, limiting further optimization. Here, we use a combined experimental and theoretical approach to provide molecular-level insight into the (de)sodiation pathways in black P anodes for sodium-ion batteries. A determination of the P binding in these materials was achieved by comparing to structure models created via species swapping, <i>ab initio</i> random structure searching, and a genetic algorithm. During sodiation, analysis of <sup>31</sup>P chemical shift anisotropies in NMR data reveals P helices and P at the end of chains as the primary structural components in amorphous Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phases. X-ray diffraction data in conjunction with variable field <sup>23</sup>Na magic-angle spinning NMR support the formation of a new Na<sub>3</sub>P crystal structure (predicted using density-functional theory) on sodiation. During desodiation, P helices are re-formed in the amorphous intermediates, albeit with increased disorder, yet emphasizing the pervasive nature of this motif. The pristine material is not re-formed at the end of desodiation and may be linked to the irreversibility observed in the Na–P system.</p></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Chemical%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Chemical structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291880&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Crystal structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Crystal%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Crystal structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291774&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nuclear%20magnetic%20resonance%20spectroscopy" class="article__tags__link">Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Physical%20and%20chemical%20processes" class="article__tags__link">Physical and chemical processes</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are promising alternatives for long-term sustainability in terms of both cost and natural abundance compared to Li-ion systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> Na is widely available and evenly distributed worldwide, lessening the political tensions that may arise from continued Li use. While numerous viable cathode materials for NIBs have been identified from Li analogues, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> many intercalation and alloying anode materials that work well for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) fail for Na chemistries. For example, the larger atomic size of Na does not allow intercalation into graphite <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> and the safety issues associated with microstructural formation are exacerbated when Na metal is used. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> When exploring materials for high capacity anodes in NIBs, elemental phosphorus is an appealing option as it exhibits the highest theoretical capacity of any anode material that has been studied, the end member composition Na<sub>3</sub>P corresponding to a capacity of 2596 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 8 and 10 to 13">(8,10−13)</a> Due to the high natural abundance of P, the use of Na–P chemistries has the potential to further drive down the cost of NIBs while achieving performances comparable to those of LIBs. Such high performing phosphorus-containing batteries have potential use in stationary systems, such as grid storage; we note, however, that the use of phosphorus anodes is associated with a safety risk—reduced phosphides can react with water, for example, to produce phosphine gas. However, with an emphasis on battery safety, monitoring tools, and quality assurance, the likelihood that these risks can be mitigated increases.</div><div class="NLM_p">Initial reports showed that anodes composed of elemental P (both the red and black allotropes) and conductive carbon can elicit high capacities in NIBs (ranging from 1479–2077 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> reversible capacity in the first cycle). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 14 to 21">(14−21)</a> Further improvements were achieved by using single-layer black P, i.e. phosphorene, in combination with graphene, which displayed a reversible capacity of 2440 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Similarly high capacities are also observed in Li-ion systems for the formation of Li<sub>3</sub>P, allowing translation into existing LIB technology. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 16 and 23 to 29">(16,23−29)</a> While these reports demonstrate the promise of P anodes for NIBs to offer high capacities competitive with LIBs, P-based anodes suffer from performance issues such as low conductivity and capacity fade during cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 30 to 33">(30−33)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Interpretation of electrochemical signatures relies on a thorough understanding of the chemistry that occurs on sodiation and desodiation. Thus, tracking the (de)sodiation pathways provides a platform with which to elucidate the structure–property relationships responsible for high capacities as well as to devise strategies to mitigate the performance degradation. Amine and co-workers <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> used <i>ex situ</i> and <i>in situ</i> synchrotron high-energy X-ray diffraction (XRD) to study electrochemical cycling of black P, and the only crystalline phases that were observed were the pristine material (orthorhombic black P) and crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>P (<i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P). No Bragg reflections were observed for any intermediate Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P (0 &lt; <i>x</i> &lt; 3) compositions studied, indicating the lack of long-range ordering, consistent with the formation of amorphous/metastable phases (<i>a</i>-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P; 0 &lt; <i>x</i> &lt; 3). Unlike other alloying anodes used in NIBs (e.g., Sn or Sb), P is a relatively light element and thus total scattering techniques such as pair distribution function (PDF) analysis of X-ray data, (which have been successfully used to aid structural assignment of both Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> both systems where disordered phases are observed along with crystalline phases) are difficult to model and interpret due to the relatively poor signal-to-noise ratio. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Conversely, NMR is well-suited to study the short-range order in the amorphous/metastable structures in <i>a</i>-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P due to the good sensitivities and 100% natural abundances of the NMR-active <sup>31</sup>P and <sup>23</sup>Na nuclei.</div><div class="NLM_p">The Na–P composition space has previously been explored by some of the authors by using density functional theory (DFT) calculations to evaluate the formation energy of experimentally known Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P structures and new structure predictions derived from <i>ab initio</i> random structure searching (AIRSS) and structural prototyping (species swapping from known phases of similar compounds). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> In similar systems, such <i>ab initio</i> structure searches have previously determined crystalline products not found in structure databases, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> as well as identifying possible structural motifs present in disordered phases formed on cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> By the nature of AIRSS and prototyping, there is, however, no information flow between calculations, and thus these techniques are limited to the initial stoichiometries chosen at their outset.</div><div class="NLM_p">Here, we show that by using a combination of solid-state NMR (ssNMR) and powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) we can monitor the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phases that are formed on (de)sodiation in black P anodes. Specifically, individual P binding sites within the amorphous Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phases that arise during cycling are probed with 2D <sup>31</sup>P phase-adjusted spinning sideband (PASS) ssNMR experiments. In 2D PASS, isotropic shifts are correlated to the corresponding anisotropic chemical shifts, facilitating analysis of the full chemical shift (CS) tensors of individual P sites and ultimately providing information on the geometry of chemical bonding environments and structures of molecular fragments. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The crystalline structures that occur are characterized by <sup>31</sup>P magic-angle spinning (MAS) ssNMR, variable field <sup>23</sup>Na MAS ssNMR, and XRD. All experimental NMR and XRD data are interpreted through first-principles computational structure prediction.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this current study, we move beyond the initial constrained AIRSS + prototype search and use a specialized variable-composition genetic algorithm (GA) code to interpolate to stoichiometries not sampled by the stochastic searches. Combining this theoretical approach with <i>ex situ</i> experimental analyses, we determine how P fragments in amorphous phases change during sodiation vs desodiation and show that the structure of the end member phase, Na<sub>3</sub>P, is a phase previously computationally predicted using the AIRSS technique. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a></div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Genetic Algorithm (GA) Sampling of Theoretical Predictions of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P Structures</h3><div class="NLM_p">The GA was initiated from low-lying AIRSS-derived phases to avoid the need to extrapolate to entirely new regions of composition space. Evaluating the fitness of child structures as a function of distance from the convex hull, the GA uncovered several motifs from less ordered phases that could not be sampled with any individual technique alone. These new motifs provided a vast library of possible P binding environments to evaluate as potential local environments and motifs that may arise in the amorphous Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P structures formed on cycling. These GA-derived phases were added to the previously constructed <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> convex hull of thermodynamically stable Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P structures in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/medium/ja-2018-04183b_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Convex hull of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub> predicted with DFT energies of known, AIRSS, or species-swapping derived phases from ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> (magenta circles) and GA-derived phases (green circles). (b) Experimental (black, C-rate of C/100) and theoretical (teal) electrochemical profiles for the sodiation of black P. The electrochemical processes on the DFT-derived profile represent the formation of the following on-hull structures: NaP<sub>7</sub> (1.47 V), Na<sub>3</sub>P<sub>11</sub> (0.99 V), Na<sub>3</sub>P<sub>7</sub> (0.96 V), NaP (0.53 V), Na<sub>5</sub>P<sub>4</sub> (0.35 V), and Na<sub>3</sub>P (0.27 V).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.8b04183&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The pristine material, black P, is a layered structure that contains six-membered P rings. The known phosphorus-rich polyphosphide of NaP<sub>7</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> was considered and found to lie on the convex hull. In NaP<sub>7</sub>, one Na coordinates to each [P<sub>7</sub>] subunit–phosphorus motifs that form infinite tubes arranged in helices about the <i>c</i> axis. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> In previous work, Morris and co-workers found a series of structures containing P cages (Na<sub>3</sub>P<sub>11</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>P<sub>7</sub> exhibit P<sub>11</sub> and P<sub>7</sub> cages, respectively) (where 0 &lt; <i>x</i> &lt; 0.43 in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P) that fall on the convex hull. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> As the sodium content increases, the next on-hull phases are NaP and Na<sub>5</sub>P<sub>4</sub>, which exhibit P helices and four-member P zig-zags, respectively. At the end of sodiation, isolated P ions are found in Na<sub>3</sub>P.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Electrochemical Characterization and Comparison with Calculated Potentials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The theoretical structure models (with energies depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a) were compared to Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phases that arise during cycling of black P in NIBs. Black P electrodes were assembled into Na half-cells for electrochemical measurements. Near-theoretical capacity was reached on the first discharge (2510 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, based on the mass of P alone) and one of the highest reversible capacities observed to date of 2074 ± 80 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> without the use of additives is achieved in the first cycle. The improved capacity may be due to the use of 1.0 M NaFSI in 2-MeTHF as the electrolyte, which differs from the electrolytes used previously (1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DMC or 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in PC). When compared to PC, methyl acetate, or THF, 2-MeTHF shows improved stability against Li, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> and similar stability is likely present toward Na, thus improving the electrochemical performance of the NIBs. The experimental P reduction profile was compared to the average voltages for on-hull Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P structures predicted by Morris and co-workers <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). When a constant current is applied (C/100), the voltage drops from the open circuit voltage to 1.10 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). This initial voltage drop suggests that P<sub>11</sub> and P<sub>7</sub> subunits, such as those found in NaP<sub>7</sub> and the computed structures of Na<sub>3</sub>P<sub>11</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>P<sub>7</sub>, may not form electrochemically during sodiation in a NIB. Their absence may be due to the unfavorable energetics involved with breaking the six-membered P rings in black P and re-forming P–P bonds to form the P<sub>11</sub> and/or P<sub>7</sub> cages in these structures. Instead a sloping region is seen between 1.10 and 0.63 V, corresponding to 31–420 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and approximate compositions of Na<sub>0.04</sub>P–Na<sub>0.49</sub>P. A steep, but short-lived sloping region is observed from 1.10–0.48 V (420–562 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, approximate compositions of Na<sub>0.50</sub>P–Na<sub>0.65</sub>P); which is followed by a more shallow, sloping region from 0.48–0.22 V (562–1358 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, approximate compositions of Na<sub>0.65</sub>P–Na<sub>1.57</sub>P), implying gradual sodiation of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P (where <i>x</i> ranges from 0.04 to 1.57) from 0.63 to 0.22 V. Following this, a plateau at 0.21 V (ranging from 1358–2340 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, where <i>x</i> corresponds to 1.57–2.70) is observed with a distinct peak in the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plot (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>), which is attributed to the formation of <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> <i>Ex Situ</i><sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR Spectra on Sodiation</h3><div class="NLM_p"><i>Ex situ</i><sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR spectra were acquired at different stages of (de)sodiation to probe the structural features of various Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phases (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>) directly. The <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR spectrum collected in the first sloping region at 0.90 V (124 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, approximate composition, Na<sub>0.14</sub>P) is nearly identical to that of the pristine material, with the exception that a weak peak at −227 ppm emerges (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> for a magnified version of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). Assignment of this low frequency resonance is not obvious based on its isotropic <sup>31</sup>P shift alone. However, we note that peaks in this region (ca. – 220 to −250 ppm) persist throughout all stages of (de)sodiation, suggesting this region arises from Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P species formed in side reactions, minor Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P intermediates or perhaps Na near P defects (such as the end of a P chain).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/medium/ja-2018-04183b_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. <i>Ex situ</i><sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR spectra (MAS = 60 kHz) of black P recorded at various stages of sodiation/desodiation (right) and the corresponding electrochemistry (left). The circles on the electrochemical profile indicate where cycling was arrested and samples were extracted for the NMR experiments. The <sup>31</sup>P chemical shift of the pristine material, black P (dark gray, 14 ppm), and the final discharge product, crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>P (light gray, –207 ppm) are shown in dashed lines. <sup>31</sup>P chemical shift regions corresponding to P helical motifs and P near the end of chains are shaded in blue and green, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.8b04183&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR spectrum for the sample extracted at 0.60 V (approximate composition Na<sub>0.52</sub>P) spans a broad chemical shift range from the resonance of the pristine material (14 ppm) to the small, sharp peak at −224 ppm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). Two pronounced broad peaks with centers-of-mass at −104 and −143 ppm are clearly resolved, these peaks becoming more pronounced as sodiation proceeds (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The corresponding <i>ex situ</i> XRD pattern of this material does not show any Bragg reflections that are distinct from the pristine material (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>) indicating that an amorphous or highly disordered phase is present in addition to residual black P. At 0.38 V (780 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>; approximately NaP; <i>x</i> = 1), a broad set of resonances spanning a <sup>31</sup>P chemical shift region from ca. –260 to 40 ppm is again observed, along with the lack of Bragg reflections (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>), suggesting that this phase is also amorphous (<i>a</i>-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P).</div><div class="NLM_p last">Following the formation of <i>a</i>-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P, a sharp <sup>31</sup>P resonance (fwhm = 10 ppm) emerges at −207 ppm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) that is coincident with the plateau at 0.21 V in the electrochemical profile and is assigned to <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P on the basis of previous <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> (and current; see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information (SI)</a>) <i>ex situ</i> XRD characterization at the corresponding state of charge. The set of broad <sup>31</sup>P resonances decrease in intensity near the end of this plateau (1666 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, approximate composition of Na<sub>1.93</sub>P) consistent with a two-phase reaction of <i>a</i>-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>P to <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P (where <i>x</i> is about 0.6; approximately composition <i>a</i>-Na<sub>2.4</sub>P), and vanish by the end of sodiation at 0.01 V (2510 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, approximate composition of Na<sub>2.90</sub>P). Here <i>x</i> was quantified based on the capacity and integrating the area corresponding to the shifts of black P (14 ppm), <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P (−207 ppm), and assuming the integral of the remaining broad resonances corresponds to <i>a</i>-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>P).</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Assignment of the Crystal Structure of <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P Formed at the End of Sodiation</h3><div class="NLM_p">Previously, the crystallographic structure of Na<sub>3</sub>P formed on discharge has been assigned as Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> on the basis of both powder <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> and single crystal <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> XRD characterizations of solid state syntheses of Na<sub>3</sub>P. However, our prior DFT calculations predicted a <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> symmetry phase to be the most stable crystal structure of <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P (i.e., located on the convex hull). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> This phase was constructed via species swapping from the Na<sub>3</sub>As structure <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> and can be rationalized as a distorted variant of a previously reported crystal structure of Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> which resides 0.005 eV·f.u.<sup>–1</sup> above the DFT hull. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> These two structural models (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a) are closely related by a group–subgroup transition of the order 6 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>). In the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> structure, the PNa<sub>5</sub> trigonal bipyramids are distorted and tilted, which can be interpreted as frozen vibrational motion of the Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> crystal structure, consistent with the anisotropic displacement parameters. The simulated XRD pattern of Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> shows additional reflections at 31.3° and 33.3° (Cu Kα) that are absent in the otherwise essentially identical pattern of Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>. Examination of the XRD of Na<sub>3</sub>P samples derived by sodiation of black P in the NIBs revealed similar superstructure reflections and thus lower symmetry (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). In addition, we find that Na<sub>3</sub>P synthesized using standard solid-state synthesis techniques in our laboratory also exhibits these superstructure reflections (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>), consistent with the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i>. A Rietveld refinement starting from the calculated structure model was performed on both samples. The refined atomic parameters for the sample synthesized via solid state synthesis differ only marginally from those calculated with DFT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S14 and S16</a>). The slightly larger deviation observed for Na<sub>3</sub>P formed electrochemically is attributed to the broadening of the Bragg reflections and thus lower signal-to-noise ratio of the data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/medium/ja-2018-04183b_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Crystal structures of Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> (left, calculated) and Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> (right). (b) <sup>23</sup>Na NMR (MAS = 60 kHz) of a sample extracted at the end of sodiation (0.01 V, 2510 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, black, bottom) compared to calculated spectra of Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> (purple, middle) and Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> (teal, top) at 7.05 (left) and 16.4 T (right). The shaded <sup>23</sup>Na peaks in the experimental spectra correspond to Na in the CMC binder, carbon mixture, and/or SEI (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). (c) PXRD patterns of NIBs (black, top) compared to calculated patterns for Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> (purple, middle, refined model) and Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> (teal, bottom). The asterisk at approximately 27, 2θ denotes the major reflection from KBr, which was used as an internal standard. Inset shows the region containing characteristic reflections for Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.8b04183&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Both structural models (<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> and <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i>) feature only one independent P position, with isolated P ions. DFT calculations of the corresponding <sup>31</sup>P NMR shifts appear in a similar spectral region (−180 vs −217 ppm for <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> vs <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>, respectively) and fall on either side of the experimentally observed value (−207 ppm). Furthermore, both crystal structures show very little anisotropy based on computed CS tensor values (anisotropy, Δ = 38 ppm and asymmetry parameter, η = 0.41 for <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i>; Δ = −39 ppm, η = 0 for <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>), making <sup>31</sup>P NMR an inconclusive diagnostic for structural determination of this species in the NIBs. However, the reduction of symmetry from <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> to <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> results in four instead of two crystallographically independent Na environments—all of which exhibit characteristic <sup>23</sup>Na quadrupolar coupling constants and asymmetry parameters (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>, <i>C</i><sub>Q</sub> and η, respectively). Comparison of the <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR of the active materials at the end of sodiation (0.01 V, 2510 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) to simulations of the <sup>23</sup>Na spectra for both structures reveal significantly better agreement with the Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b), supporting the assignment of the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> in NIBs.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Structural Assignment of P Motifs in Amorphous Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P (<i>x</i> = 1) During Sodiation</h3><div class="NLM_p">With assignment of the crystallographic structure of the final discharge product in hand, the structures of the amorphous intermediates that form during (de)sodiation are now investigated in order to assign the observed <sup>31</sup>P NMR resonances to specific local structures. [N.B. <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR spectra were acquired for all the samples studied (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) and compared with DFT calculations of on-hull structures, but they were less informative than the <sup>31</sup>P spectra (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>).] NMR parameters for energetically low-lying compositions corresponding to stoichiometries at or near Na<sub>1</sub>P<sub>1</sub> (NaP, Na<sub>5</sub>P<sub>6</sub>, Na<sub>5</sub>P<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>7</sub>P<sub>8</sub>, Na<sub>8</sub>P<sub>7</sub>) were first calculated (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). The structural diversity computationally accessed in this composition range from known, AIRSS, prototype, and GA-derived structures allows us to sample the <sup>31</sup>P site variation that may be observed in the amorphous Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phases more fully. Unfortunately, the <sup>31</sup>P isotropic shifts calculated for these structural models were associated with a narrow spectral range, or the materials themselves did not exhibit a bandgap (which did not allow us to calculate accurate values for δ<sub>iso</sub> because the Knight shift contribution that describes the influence of free carriers could not be computed), prohibiting assignment based on isotropic shift alone. However, we found that individual <sup>31</sup>P sites within the structural models exhibited distinct differences in chemical shift anistropies (CSAs). Therefore, local <sup>31</sup>P environments in the <i>a</i>-NaP phase were examined in greater detail with 2D <sup>31</sup>P PASS experiments to separate isotropic and anisotropic chemical shifts and thus enable analysis of the full CS tensors of individual P sites. 2D PASS is particularly useful here, not only because of ambiguity in the isotropic <sup>31</sup>P dimension, but these spectra are also broadened by strong intramolecular <sup>31</sup>P–<sup>31</sup>P dipolar interactions <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> – potential interferences that are eliminated in the anisotropic dimension in PASS.</div><div class="NLM_p">Deconvolution of the <sup>31</sup>P isotropic projection shows the presence of (at least) three environments with associated <sup>31</sup>P shifts at −29, –78, and −143 ppm that correspond to <i>a</i>-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P species (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a), (with anisotropic projections shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b–d), the width and asymmetry of the CSA patterns differing noticeably for the three resonances. Sideband patterns were simulated in dmfit <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b–d, gray) and compared to those calculated for <sup>31</sup>P sites in DFT models (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b–d, purple) with similar compositions (<i>vide supra</i>).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/medium/ja-2018-04183b_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Deconvolution of the <sup>31</sup>P isotropic projection from 2D <sup>31</sup>P PASS experiments (black line) performed on a sample of approximate composition NaP, extracted from a NIB at 0.38 V (780 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) during sodiation. Five regions with centers-of-mass at 14 (black P), −29, −78, −143, and −235 ppm are observed. The fit of the line shape from deconvolution is shown in gray. (b–d) Corresponding experimental anisotropic projections from 2D <sup>31</sup>P PASS (black, bottom) showing sideband patterns for δ<sub>iso</sub> = −29 (b), −78 (c), and −143 ppm (d) with the respective fits (middle, gray) compared to sideband patterns calculated for individual P sites in DFT models (top, purple) at MAS = 10 kHz. The corresponding P sites (top) and extended P structures (bottom) are displayed to the right of each sideband pattern and are listed in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.8b04183&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The <sup>31</sup>P site at δ<sub>iso</sub> = −29 ppm agrees well with the CS tensor found for one crystallographically independent P site (P2 of P1–P2) in a metastable phase of NaP + 96 meV above the hull (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>), which contains P arranged in a 3-fold helix that winds through the Na network. (In general, we find that all P atoms that comprise helical fragments are bound to two P atoms and surrounded by four to six Na atoms.) The P1 site of this phase is associated with a chemical shift of −57 ppm, and if present, would likely be obscured by the much more intense δ<sub>iso</sub> = −78 ppm resonance. Similarly, the <sup>31</sup>P environment at δ<sub>iso</sub> = −78 ppm agrees with the CS tensor found for the third independent P site (P3, of P1–P7) in NaP + 75 meV above the hull that also contains P helices (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>) but has very different CSA parameters. The <sup>31</sup>P site at δ<sub>iso</sub> = −143 ppm closely resembles that of the fourth independent P site (P4, of P1–P8) in a second NaP structure + 96 meV above the hull, found via GA (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>), this site corresponding to a terminal unit of a four-member P zig-zag motif, suggesting that this site represents a terminal unit of a P chain (i.e., site of P–P bond cleavage). Although there are exceptions, Δ is generally larger for sites at the end of P chains or in small clusters, in all the calculated structures examined, again supporting this assignment. It is important to stress that we do not believe that the various crystalline structures are formed, simply that CSA parameters consistent with terminal and helical structures appear to be present in the amorphous phase. We note that (in contrast to the one pulse spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) a well-defined peak for δ<sub>iso</sub> = −104 ppm is not observed in the isotropic projection from 2D PASS. The absence of this and other possible resonances may be due to short spin–spin (<i>T</i><sub>2</sub>) relaxation (all sites show <i>T</i><sub>2</sub> values &lt;1 ms) with respect to the rotor period (100 μs) that significantly lowers the signal over the course of the five consecutive π pulses that are applied during the PASS experiment. Thus, while we can identify individual <sup>31</sup>P sites from different structural models that show similar CS tensors to the experimental PASS data, the remaining sites in these helical units may also not be observed due to fast <i>T</i><sub>2</sub> relaxation and subsequent dephasing and/or overlap in the isotropic projection.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. Comparison of the Experimental <sup>31</sup>P CS Tensors Obtained for the “NaP” Sample Extracted at 0.38 V during Sodiation and Calculated CS Tensor Parameters for Individual <sup>31</sup>P Sites in Structural Models with Stoichiometries at or near NaP</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char"
