site/compound</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="​">δ<sub>iso</sub> (ppm)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">Δ (ppm)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">η</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">experimental</td><td class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">P2 in NaP + 96 meV above hull<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t2fn1" aria-label="a"><sup>a</sup></a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="​">–21</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">171</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.55</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" colspan="4" scope="row" align="left">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">experimental</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="​">–78</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–261</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.75</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">P3 in NaP + 75 meV above hull<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t2fn2" aria-label="b"><sup>b</sup></a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" align="char" char="​">–143</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–432</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.87</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">P4 in NaP + 96 meV above hull<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t2fn2" aria-label="b"><sup>b</sup></a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="​">92<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t2fn3" aria-label="c"><sup>c</sup></a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–438</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.85</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t2fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t2fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">Structural model was found using AIRSS.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t2fn2"><sup><sup>b</sup></sup><p class="last">Model was found using GA.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t2fn3"><sup><sup>c</sup></sup><p class="last">Model does not exhibit a bandgap, and the Knight shift contribution to the isotropic shift was not calculated.</p></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p last">Similar, broad <sup>31</sup>P shifts near −29, −78, and −143 ppm remain in the <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR spectra during further sodiation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). Specifically, the resonance near −143 ppm increases in intensity relative to the other broad peaks (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>), suggesting further fragmentation of P helices as more Na is added to the system. Analysis of the chemical shift tensor for the site at −235 ppm at 0.38 V (780 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>; approximately NaP; <i>x</i> = 1, <i>vide infra</i>) reveals a small anisotropy value (Δ = 93 ppm, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>) that is similar to the isotropic <sup>31</sup>P shifts and anisotropies associated with isolated P ions (e.g., δ<sub>iso</sub> = −180 ppm, Δ = 38 ppm for <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> and δ<sub>iso</sub> = −217 ppm, Δ = −39 ppm for <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>).</div></div><div id="sec2_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Structural Assignment of P Motifs in <i>a</i>-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P (<i>x</i> = 1) during Desodiation</h3><div class="NLM_p">During desodiation, the <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i><sup>31</sup>P resonance at −207 ppm persists during desodiation until &gt;0.60 V (&gt;1490 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, approximate composition of Na<sub>1.28</sub>P), allowing the assignment of the disappearance of <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P to the peak at 0.60 V in the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plot (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). 2D <sup>31</sup>P PASS experiments were again performed on the NaP (<i>x</i> = 1) stoichiometry sample (0.80 V, 1730 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), for comparison to the P environments in <i>a</i>-NaP formed on sodiation. The <sup>31</sup>P peak at δ<sub>iso</sub> = −143 ppm exhibits lower intensity in the isotropic projection (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>), compared to the MAS spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>), indicating a change in the <i>T</i><sub>2</sub> relaxation behavior of this <sup>31</sup>P site on desodiation and/or overlap with a site that exhibits shorter <i>T</i><sub>2</sub> relaxation. The sideband pattern from 2D PASS for δ<sub>iso</sub> = −143 ppm could not be reliably simulated with a single CSA pattern (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>); this may be due to overlapping <sup>31</sup>P sites at this frequency and/or increased structural disorder (either motional or static). These data suggest that although a clear <sup>31</sup>P peak is observed at −143 ppm in the NMR spectra collected at 0.60 and 0.80 V (1490 and 1730 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) on desodiation, this may be due to different P sites that exhibit fortuitous overlap at this chemical shift and/or that P atoms at the end of a chain may exhibit more variation in coordination environment.</div><div class="NLM_p">The remaining <sup>31</sup>P sideband patterns from 2D PASS experiments on <i>a</i>-NaP (0.8 V, 1730 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, approximately NaP) during desodiation can again be correlated with P motifs in the structural models (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">3</a>), the environments with δ<sub>iso</sub> = −29 ppm and −78 ppm again showing reasonable agreement with NaP structures containing helices. The CSA parameters, while similar to those observed on sodiation, are closer to those of structures with longer average Na–P interatomic distances (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a> for an expanded description of the structure models). Increased structural disorder on desodiation may be due to a larger range of clusters that are formed from the isolated P ions in Na<sub>3</sub>P, as opposed to the original P phase, which already contains P–P bonds. At the end of desodiation at 2.5 V, a <sup>31</sup>P shift (2 ppm) close to that of the pristine material (14 ppm) reemerges but is present alongside broad <sup>31</sup>P resonances that span from 2 to −240 ppm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b), indicating that the Na ions are not completely removed during desodiation and/or are consumed in deleterious side reactions. <i>Ex situ</i> XRD collected at the end of sodiation shows that black P is not re-formed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>). Rather, only one reflection at 26° that is also seen in <i>c</i>-black P is observed, suggesting that the material becomes increasingly amorphous during cycling.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/medium/ja-2018-04183b_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Experimental anisotropic projections from 2D <sup>31</sup>P PASS (black, bottom) of NIBs at 0.80 V (1730 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) during desodiation showing sideband patterns for δ<sub>iso</sub> = −29 (a) and −78 ppm (b) with the respective fits (middle, gray) compared to sideband patterns calculated for individual P sites in DFT models (top, purple) at MAS = 10 kHz. The corresponding P sites (top) and extended P structures (bottom) are displayed to the right of each sideband pattern and are listed in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">3</a>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/large/ja-2018-04183b_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.8b04183&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl3"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 3. <sup>31</sup>P Sites in Structural Models with Stoichiometries at or near NaP</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col bandgap, and the Knight shift contribution to the isotropic shift was not calculated.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t3fn3"><sup><sup>c</sup></sup><p class="last">Simulation does not accurately represent experimental data due to spectral overlap.</p></div></div></div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">From the experimental and theoretical data presented, we propose a mechanism for sodiation/desodiation in phosphorus anodes for NIBs. <sup>31</sup>P NMR spectra acquired during the initial stages of sodiation (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P, <i>x</i> = 0.14) are similar to those of the pristine material, consistent with DFT calculations performed by Hembram et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> which showed that the P–P bonds remained intact at low levels of sodiation (<i>x</i> &lt; 0.25). Only the interlayer distances increase, allowing Na ion intercalation prior to alloying and fragmentation of black P. The P<sub>11</sub> and P<sub>7</sub> cages present in on-hull Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phases are not observed here, likely due to the energetic penalty associated with breaking/re-forming P–P bonds required to make P cages. Importantly, our <sup>31</sup>P NMR spectra at early stages of sodiation do not resemble those reported for NaP<sub>7</sub>, which shows four <sup>31</sup>P resonances at −32.9, 20.6, 30.5, and 68.5 ppm that arise from the four independent <sup>31</sup>P sites in this compound. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Once the composition reaches approximately Na<sub>0.52</sub>P, <sup>31</sup>P NMR shifts consistent with P helices/zig-zags are present, suggesting that at this stage, the initial P–P cleavage has occurred, and P helices are the predominant structural motifs that are subsequently formed. P helices and zig-zags (which are likely truncated helices) may allow some of the original alternating planar/staggered P–P bonds found in pristine black P to persist while also forming an energetically favorable Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phase.</div><div class="NLM_p">As black P is further sodiated, the amorphous Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phase that forms at 0.38 V (780 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, approximate composition of NaP) shows <sup>31</sup>P sites consistent with P helices and terminal P units from short P chains (four-member P zig-zags). The calculated voltage profile is in agreement with the measured experimental profile for <i>x</i> &gt; 0.5, again confirming that the structural (helical) motifs found in structures such as NaP and Na<sub>5</sub>P<sub>4</sub> close to the convex hull are likely present.</div><div class="NLM_p">Crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>P emerges during a two-phase reaction that occurs below 0.22 V, suggesting that isolated P ions are derived from the P helices and shortened P chains (which increase in intensity in samples extracted during the two-phase reaction, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8</a>). A new crystalline architecture of Na<sub>3</sub>P (<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i>) was discovered from species swapping with Na<sub>3</sub>As that is more thermodynamically stable than the previously determined Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> structure found in two independent reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> Theoretical calculations, variable field <sup>23</sup>Na NMR, and XRD are consistent with the formation of this phase in a NIB and in solid-state syntheses performed in our laboratory. Crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>P persists in the batteries during desodiation until a potential &gt;0.6 V (&gt;1490 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) is reached. In the <i>a</i>-NaP phase accessed during desodiation at 0.80 V (1730 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), more disordered P helices are found, perhaps due to the increased structural disorder associated with forming helices from isolated P ions in <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i>. Black P does not re-form on Na removal from the system, and this irreversible chemistry may play a role in the poor capacity retention.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The prevalence of P helices in <i>a</i>-NaP during sodiation/desodiation indicates that this motif is ubiquitous in amorphous/metastable Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P structures. Four-fold P helices are present in <i>c</i>-NaP, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> which falls on the convex hull tie-line, suggesting that this structural motif is conserved in the amorphous analogue, where more disorder and thus, lower symmetry is found. Calculations show that the higher symmetry <i>c</i>-NaP is associated with similar isotropic and anisotropic shifts for both independent <sup>31</sup>P sites in the structure (P1, δ<sub>iso</sub> = −179 ppm, Δ = 288 ppm, and η = 0.40; P2, δ<sub>iso</sub> = −145 ppm, Δ = 292 ppm, and η = 0.59), neither of which accurately represent the experimental data, suggesting that lower symmetry/more disordered helices are present. DFT calculations by Hembram et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> suggested that P dumbbells are formed at a composition of Na<sub>0.28</sub>P that continue to increase in quantity until individual P ions are observed in Na<sub>3</sub>P. In other Li- <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref49 ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref49 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 39 and 49 to 51">(39,49−51)</a> and Na-ion <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 34 and 35">(34,35)</a> Sn, Ge and Si alloying anodes, dumbbell motifs are commonly observed in the amorphous phases formed on cycling. Conversely, our calculations show that the lowest lying structure of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P (<i>x</i> = 2) that exhibits dumbbells falls +54 meV above the hull, indicating that dumbbell-containing structures are less stable in the Na–P system. To determine whether dumbbell motifs were also formed during the (de)sodiation of black P, we compared the experimental CS tensors to those found for P–P dumbbells in Na<sub>2</sub>P models with calculated energies ranging from +54 to +199 meV above the hull: while dumbbell motifs are associated with similar asymmetry parameters and sign of anisotropy for some of the <sup>31</sup>P sites found on sodiation, the magnitude of the anisotropy is consistently smaller for the dumbbell motifs than those observed experimentally and the isotropic shift values deviate dramatically (e.g., experimental values for δ<sub>iso</sub> = −29 ppm, Δ = 179 ppm, and η = 0.72 whereas a dumbbell motif exhibits calculated values of δ<sub>iso</sub> = −246 ppm, Δ = 171 ppm, and η = 0.68, ultimately indicating better agreement with the P site in helices with δ<sub>iso</sub> = −21 ppm, Δ = 171 ppm, and η = 0.55, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). The lack of experimental evidence combined with the unfavorable formation energies of Na<sub>2</sub>P structures indicates that dumbbell-containing structures do not form in a measurable quantity in these NIBs during cycling. Conversely, the CS tensor for δ<sub>iso</sub> = −143 ppm is consistent with the terminal P atom in a four-member zig-zag motif, suggesting that this environment may represent the P–P cleavage site of a helix/zig-zag.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Overall, we followed the structural transformations that occur during cycling in high capacity black P anodes for NIBs with solid-state NMR, XRD, and DFT calculations, providing insight into the (de)sodiation pathway. In tracking the various Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P intermediates that formed on cycling, the discovery of new Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P structures via the GA was crucial to assigning the P motifs present in <i>a</i>-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P intermediates and AIRSS + prototyping structure searches allowed the assignment of the final discharge product.</div><div class="NLM_p last">During the first sodiation, P atoms at the end of a chain are observed as early as 0.60 V (Na<sub>0.52</sub>P), indicating that P–P cleavage begins at this composition. In <i>a</i>-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P, P motifs that correspond to both P helices and the terminal unit in P zig-zags are observed, suggesting a sodiation mechanism that is distinct from other alloying anodes including other group 15 elements, e.g. Sb, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> in which dumbbells are present. Once the potential drops below 0.22 V, the formation of a lower energy <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> structure, which was predicted to form <i>a priori</i>, appears and persists during desodiation until &gt;0.60 V. By comparing the product formed at the end of sodiation in NIBs with traditional solid-state synthesis, we find that the crystalline architecture is Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> in both cases, indicating that both routes access the thermodynamically favorable structure. Slight differences in P environments in <i>a</i>-NaP during sodiation/desodiation may result from different pathways that are accessed to form extended P structures from the pristine black P vs the isolated P atoms in <i>c</i>-Na<sub>3</sub>P-<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i>, respectively. At the end of desodiation, we find that black P is not re-formed, which may contribute to the poor capacity retention in this material. The combined approach of analyzing both experimental and theoretical CSA can be extended to understand other structural transformations on (de)lithiation/(de)sodiation in not only phosphorus-containing materials, but also systems, such as Si, where distinct structural motifs, such as clusters and chains, play an important role in the battery chemistry of these largely amorphous phases.</div></div><div id="sec5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i15">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec5_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i16" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i16"> Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Red phosphorus (≥99.99%), carbon nanotubes (multiwalled), graphite (99.95%), 2-methyl tetrahydrofuran (2-MeTHF, anhydrous, ≥99.0%), sodium metal (cubes in mineral oil, 99.9%), hexane (anhydrous, 95%), and sodium carboxymethyl cellulose (NaCMC, DS = 0.7, <i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 700 000) were purchased from Sigma-Aldrich. Carbon super P (Timcal, 99+%) was purchased from Alfa Aesar. Sodium(I) bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI, 99.7%) was purchased from Solvionic. Prior to use, NaFSI was dried at 100 °C under vacuum before transferring to an Ar-filled glovebox (M. Braun; <i>p</i>(O<sub>2</sub>)/<i>p</i><sup>0</sup> &lt; 1 ppm, <i>p</i>(H<sub>2</sub>O)/<i>p</i><sup>0</sup> &lt; 1 ppm). All other reagents were used as received.</div></div><div id="sec5_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i17" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i17"> Electrode Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Red phosphorus powder (400 mg, 13 mmol) was placed in a ZrO<sub>2</sub> jar in an Ar-filled glovebox and ball-milled using a SPEX 8000 M mixer/mill for 4 h to produce crystalline black phosphorus. To make the electrodes, black P was combined with conductive carbon for a final mass ratio of P:C = 50:50 (where C = 15 graphite:10 carbon nanotubes:10 carbon super P:15 NaCMC). First, black P was mixed with graphite, carbon nanotubes, and carbon super P in a ZrO<sub>2</sub> jar and ball-milled for 1 h under Ar. After ball-milling, NaCMC in water was added to the P:C mixture to form an aqueous slurry which was cast onto etched Cu foil with a 150 μm doctor blade. The resulting film was dried in air overnight, followed by drying at 100 °C under vacuum for an additional 12 h. Supported black P electrodes for assembly in coin cells were made using a 12.7 mm diameter circular punch and used for all electrochemical measurements and <i>ex situ</i> characterization studies with NMR and XRD. Typical loading values of active material were 1–3 mg of black P per electrode.</div></div><div id="sec5_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i18" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i18"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Black P electrodes were assembled into Na half-cells using R2032 coin cells in an Ar-filled glovebox. The electrolyte used for all measurements was 1.0 M NaFSI dissolved in 2-MeTHF. Black P and metallic Na were separated with a glass fiber separator (Whatman, GF/A). All electrochemical measurements were performed using a Biologic MPG2 battery cycler. Cells were galvanostatically cycled in the potential range of 2.5 to 0.01 V at a rate of C/100, unless otherwise noted. C-rate is defined as the inverse of the number of hours it takes to reach a defined theoretical capacity—in this case, 2596 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec5_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i19" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i19"> Solid-state NMR Spectroscopy</h3><div class="NLM_p">For <i>ex situ</i> NMR measurements, black P electrodes were cycled against Na in half-cells to various states of charge. Batteries were disassembled in an Ar-filled glovebox and active materials were scraped off the Cu foil and washed twice with 2-MeTHF before drying under vacuum for 30 min. The dried powder (approximately 3–8 mg of material) was packed into ZrO<sub>2</sub> rotors for analysis with <sup>31</sup>P and <sup>23</sup>Na solid-state NMR. Dried powders filled 1.3 mm o.d. (2.5 μL internal volume) ZrO<sub>2</sub> rotors for fast MAS experiments. For 2D <sup>31</sup>P PASS measurements, 3–8 mg of material from a single battery could not fill the entire volume of a 4 mm o.d. ZrO<sub>2</sub> rotor (80 μL internal volume). For these experiments, material collected from a single battery was center-packed using Teflon to fill the void space. <sup>31</sup>P and <sup>23</sup>Na shifts were externally referenced to solid ammonium dihydrogen phosphate at 0.8 ppm and solid sodium chloride at 7.2 ppm, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">Solid-state <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR measurements were performed on Bruker Avance III 300 and 700 MHz spectrometers (operating at 7.05 and 16.4 T, respectively) equipped with a 1.3 mm HX MAS probehead. <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR experiments were collected at MAS frequency = 60 kHz using a rotor-synchronized quadrupolar echo sequence and a recycle delay of 5 s (≥5 × <i>T</i><sub>1</sub>). Solid-state <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR measurements were performed on a Bruker Avance III 300 MHz spectrometer (7.05 T) equipped with a 1.3 mm HX probehead. Rotor-synchronized Hahn echo <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR experiments were collected at MAS frequency = 60 kHz with a recycle delay of 10 s (at least 5 × <i>T</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> values ranged from hundreds of ms to approximately 2 s) [N.B. A recycle delay of 60 s was used for the pristine black P due to the longer spin–lattice relaxation time (<i>T</i><sub>1</sub> = 9.5 s) observed for this sample.]</div><div class="NLM_p">2D <sup>31</sup>P PASS NMR experiments were performed on a Bruker Avance III 500 spectrometer operating at 11.74 T and equipped with a 4 mm HXY MAS probehead. Spinning speeds of 10 kHz and recycle delays of 10 s were used for all PASS experiments. The PASS pulse sequence consists of a π/2 pulse followed by a train of five rotor-synchronized π pulses with interpulse delays that satisfy the PASS equations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> For each experiment, the indirect dimension was incremented in 16 steps, with either 1200 or 2400 scans collected per step. All 2D data sets were processed by repeating the 2D signal in the indirect dimension eight times to separate spinning sidebands. All spinning sideband patterns were simulated in dmfit <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> to extract the principal components (δ<sub>11</sub>, δ<sub>22</sub>, and δ<sub>33</sub>) of the chemical shift tensor for comparison to DFT models. All chemical shift tensors reported here use the Haeberlen–Mehring convention <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> to define the isotropic chemical shift (δ<sub>iso</sub>), anisotropy (Δ), and asymmetry (η) as follows:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/medium/ja-2018-04183b_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i20"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/medium/ja-2018-04183b_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i21"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/medium/ja-2018-04183b_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i22"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p last">In the Haeberlen convention, the principal components are defined as follows:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/asset/images/medium/ja-2018-04183b_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i23"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></div></div><div id="sec5_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i24" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i24"> X-ray Diffraction</h3><div class="NLM_p">The air-sensitive Na<sub>3</sub>P powder samples were finely ground in an agate mortar, filled into 0.3 mm diameter glass capillaries, and sealed with two-component glue inside an Ar-filled glovebox. Diffraction data was collected at 296(2) K on a Panalytical Empyrean diffractometer equipped with a Ni filter using Cu Kα radiation (λ = 154.06 pm, 154.43 pm). Rietveld refinement was performed using the TOPAS Academic software package (version 4.1). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> Due to inadequate data quality, the displacement parameters of the atoms were fixed to <i>U</i><sub>iso</sub> = 0.013 pm<sup>2</sup>. In addition, the <i>z</i> parameter of one atom needed to be fixed owing to strong correlation. After individual refinement of each <i>z</i> parameter while one other was fixed, <i>z</i>(P1) was fixed for the last iteration. Interatomic distances should be treated with care due to the data quality. Crystal structures were visualized using the Diamond software package. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Data in brief for Na<sub>3</sub>P at 296(2) K: <i>a</i> = 861.10(2) pm, <i>c</i> = 881.88(2) pm, <i>V</i> = 566.30(2) ·10<sup>6</sup> pm<sup>3</sup>, <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>cm</i> (no. 185), <i>Z</i> = 6, ρ<sub>calc</sub> = 1.76 g cm<sup>–3</sup>, μ(Cu Kα) = 7.61 mm<sup>–1</sup>, <i>h</i> ≤ 7, <i>k</i> ≤ 7, <i>l</i> ≤ 8, 10° ≤ 2θ ≤ 90°, 4705 points, 32 parameters, <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 0.037, <i>R</i><sub>exp</sub> = 0.023, <i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 0.027, GOF = 1.593, <i>R</i><sub>Bragg</sub> = 0.015. Further details of the crystal structure determination are available from the Fachinformationszentrum Karlsruhe, D-76344 Eggenstein-Leopoldshafen, Germany, E-mail: <a href="mailto:crysdata@fiz-karlsruhe.de">crysdata@fiz-karlsruhe.de</a>, on quoting the depository numbers CSD-433838 (Na<sub>3</sub>P).</div></div><div id="sec5_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i25" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i25"> Density Functional Theory and Computational Structure Predictions</h3><div class="NLM_p">Following the comprehensive structure prediction study of Mayo et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> a parallel, cross-compositional genetic algorithm (GA) was used to enhance the sampling of metastable Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phases. Specifically targeting the region 1 – δ &lt; <i>x</i> &lt; 2 + δ, new low-lying phases were discovered by extrapolating the local structures found using <i>ab initio</i> random structure searching (AIRSS) to larger cells of nontrivial stoichiometries via simple cut-and-splice crossover (70%) and mutation (30%). Aside from physicality of density and bond lengths, two criteria were enforced upon each trial structure; the unit cell must contain fewer than 40 atoms, and it must be unique to other computed structures (as defined via overlap of pair distribution functions). Each trial structure was relaxed with density functional theory (DFT) forces using the planewave pseudopotential code CASTEP (version 16.1) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> such that formation energies were converged to within 10 meV·atom<sup>–1</sup>. This level of accuracy required a planewave cutoff of 400 eV and a Monkhorst–Pack grid with spacing finer than 2π × 0.05 Å<sup>–1</sup> to sample the Brillouin zone. The fitness of each structure was evaluated as a logistic function of the distance from the convex hull of previously computed formation energies; fitness proportionate (roulette wheel) selection was used to propagate structures to the next generation, of which 50% were selected from elite structures of earlier generations. Several GA runs were performed with a generation size of 30 relaxed structures, until further generations no longer explored new regions of configuration space.</div><div class="NLM_p">The 318 unique structures that were found to lie less than 150 meV·atom<sup>–1</sup> from the convex hull were re-relaxed using the exchange–correlation functional of Perdew, Burke, and Ernzerhof (PBE) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> and Vanderbilt ultrasoft pseudopotentials (generated on-the-fly with Na: 2|1.3|1.3|1.0|16|19|21|20U:30U:21(qc = 8), P: 2|1.8|1.8|1.5|3|5|6|30:31:32LGG). Convergence of formation energies to within 1 meV·atom<sup>–1</sup> required truncation of the planewave basis set at 650 eV, and Brillouin zone sampling with a Monkhorst–Pack grid finer than 2π × 0.03 Å<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">The original work of Mayo et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> searched for candidate crystal structures using a combination of the AIRSS and species-swapping structure prediction methods. These techniques have been successfully applied in several previous studies of battery materials, such as in Li–Sn and Li–Sb, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> Na–Sn, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> and in Li-FeS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> As well as the structures arising from our GA searches, we also examined the structures lying within 100 meV·atom<sup>–1</sup> of the convex hull in reference  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> with stoichiometries NaP, Na<sub>2</sub>P, Na<sub>5</sub>P<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>5</sub>P<sub>6</sub> for our NMR calculations.</div></div><div id="sec5_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i26" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i26"> NMR Calculations</h3><div class="NLM_p">Chemical shifts and other NMR parameters were calculated using the gauge-including-projector-augmented-wave (GIPAW) algorithm and the Quantum Espresso code, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> with the GIPAW pseudopotentials Na.pbe-tm-gipaw-dc.UPF and P.pbe-tm-new-gipaw-dc.UPF, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> and the PBE exchange–correlation functional. A kinetic-energy cutoff of 1360 and 5442 eV was used for the plane-wave expansion of the Kohn–Sham orbitals and the charge density, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">Structures for the NMR calculations were first relaxed using DFT and the CASTEP or Quantum Espresso codes, using a Brillouin zone sampling density of 2π × 0.025 Å<sup>–1</sup>. Following this, an NMR calculation was carried out with the aforementioned parameters and pseudopotentials using a stricter sampling density of 2π × 0.008 Å<sup>–1</sup>. With this sampling density, we estimate that total NMR chemical shifts are converged to ±1 ppm. We found that this density is necessary as some of our candidate crystal structures display semimetallic or metallic behavior under DFT.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Additional data related to this publication are available at the Cambridge data repository. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a></div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i27">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/jacs.8b04183" class="ext-link">10.1021/jacs.8b04183</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Additional electrochemistry, solid-state NMR spectroscopy, PXRD, and structural modeling data, including Figures S1–S17 and Tables S1 and S2 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.8b04183/suppl_file/ja8b04183_si_001.pdf">ja8b04183_si_001.pdf (1.8 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.8b04183" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy d. is the main property of rechargeable batteries that has driven the entire technol. forward in past decades.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) now surpass other, previously competitive battery types (for example, lead-acid and nickel metal hydride) but still require extensive further improvement to, in particular, extend the operation hours of mobile IT devices and the driving mileages of all-elec. vehicles.  In this Review, we present a crit. overview of a wide range of post-LIB materials and systems that could have a pivotal role in meeting such demands.  We divide battery systems into two categories: near-term and long-term technologies.  To provide a realistic and balanced perspective, we describe the operating principles and remaining issues of each post-LIB technol., and also evaluate these materials under com. cell configurations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpmrufRRuizrLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg87aPKm5qbKw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVert7k%253D&amp;md5=3d2782c5ebf801e43442f01f2206379f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1149/2.0151514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Research on electrochem. Na intercalation in battery system has been reported since the early 1980s but Na-ion batteries are not commercialized so far though studies on Li-ion batteries have been reported since the late 1970s and the practical batteries have been extensively utilized for portable device applications in the world since 1991.  Now, targeted application of research and development for rechargeable batteries has changed toward realization of the sustainable energy society.  With the change in social situation and development of the battery technol., studies on Na-ion batteries have been attracted significant interests since 2010.  Although research interests of the electrode materials for Na-ion batteries are evoked in many researchers, advantages, disadvantages, and issues are not fully discussed for realizing the commercialization of Na-ion batteries.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg87aPKm5qbKw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/smtd.201700098"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mani, U.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, H. In this article, we review the up-to-date research progress on anode materials for Na-ion batteries from five respects: carbon-based materials, alloy-based materials, metal oxides and sulfides based on conversion reaction, titanium-based compds. with insertion mechanism, and org. composites.  In particular, we not only summarize the Na-storage mechanism of these anodes, but also discuss the failure mechanism.  The problems and challenges assocd. with these anodes are pointed out.  Furthermore, on the basis of extensive reports and our exptl. studies, feasible strategies are suggested for designing high performance anode materials.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Capacity Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Youngjin; Ha, Kwang-Ho; Oh, Seung M.; Lee, Kyu Tae</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>

                10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DLuo%252C%2BW.%253B%2BShen%252C%2BF.%253B%2BBommier%252C%2BC.%253B%2BZhu%252C%2BH.%253B%2BJi%252C%2BX.%253B%2BHu%252C%2BL.%2BAcc.%2BChem.%2BRes.%2B2016%252C%2B49%252C%2B231%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Fjacs.8b04183&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.accounts.5b00482&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljKElDgi_9tjQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06467D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent developments in electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Luyuan Paul; Yu, Linghui; Wang, Xin; Srinivasan, Madhavi; Xu, Zhichuan J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9353-9378</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The rapid consumption of nonrenewable resources resulted in an ever-increasing problem of CO2 emissions that has motivated people for studying the harvesting of energy from renewable alternatives (e.g. solar and wind).  Efficient electrochem. energy storage devices play a crucial role in storing harvested energies in the daily lives.  For example, rechargeable batteries can store energy generated by solar cells during the daytime and release it during night-time.  In particular, Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have received considerable attention ever since their early commercialization in 1990s.  However, with initiatives by several governments to build large-scale energy grids to store energy for cities, problems such as the high cost and limited availability of Li starts to become major issues.  Na, which also belongs to Group 1 of the periodic table, has comparable electrochem. properties to Li, and more importantly it is considerably more accessible than Li.  Nonetheless, research into Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is currently still in its infancy compared to LIBs, although great leaps and bounds have been made recently in terms of research and development into this technol.  Here in this review, the authors summarize the recent advancements made, also covering the prospective materials for both the battery cathode and anode.  Addnl., opinions on possible solns. through correlating trends in recent papers will be suggested.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqaYAOgglkaCrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljKElDgi_9tjQ"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Phosphorus-carbon nanocomposite anodes for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ramireddy, Thrinathreddy; Xing, Tan; Rahman, Md Mokhlesur; Chen, Ying; Dutercq, Quentin; Gunzelmann, Daniel; Glushenkov, Alexey M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5572-5584</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">With the expected theor. capacity of 2596 mA h g-1, P is considered to be the highest capacity anode material for Na-ion batteries and one of the most attractive anode materials for Li-ion systems.  This work presents a comprehensive study of P-C nanocomposite anodes for both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries.  The composite electrodes are able to display high initial capacities of ∼1700 and 1300 mA h g-1 in Li and Na half-cells, resp., when the cells are tested within a larger potential windows of 2.0-0.01 V vs. Li/Li+ and Na/Na+.  The level of demonstrated capacity is underpinned by the storage mechanism, based on the transformation of P to Li3P phase for Li cells and an incomplete transformation to Na3P phase for Na cells.  The capacity deteriorates upon cycling, which is shown to originate from disintegration of electrodes and their delamination from current collectors by post-cycling ex situ electron microscopy.  Stable cyclic performance at the level of ∼700 and ∼350-400 mA h g-1 can be achieved if the potential windows are restricted to 2.0-0.67 V vs. Li/Li+ for Li and 2-0.33 vs. Na/Na+ for Na half-cells.  The results are critically discussed in light of literature reports.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpbpTdmNtgwtrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgOHHqd5bqjkA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXit1aqt7o%253D&amp;md5=964498820e44c8f37b2e4e369def7076</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1021/nl502759z"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gordin, M.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A phosphorene-graphene hybrid material as a high-capacity anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jie; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Pasta, Mauro; Yuan, Hongtao; Zheng, Guangyuan; Sun, Yongming; Li, Yuzhang; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">980-985</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have recently attracted significant attention as an alternative to Li-ion batteries because Na sources do not present the geopolitical issues that Li sources might.  Although recent reports on cathode materials for Na-ion batteries demonstrated performances comparable to their Li-ion counterparts, the major scientific challenge for a competitive Na-ion battery technol. is to develop viable anode materials.  A hybrid material made out of a few phosphorene layers sandwiched between graphene layers shows a specific capacity of 2 440 mA h g-1 (calcd. using the mass of P only) at a c.d. of 0.05 A g-1 and an 83% capacity retention after 100 cycles while operating between 0 and 1.5 V.  Using in situ TEM and ex situ x-ray diffraction techniques, the authors explain the large capacity of the anode through a dual mechanism of intercalation of Na ions along the x axis of the phosphorene layers followed by the formation of a Na3P alloy.  The presence of graphene layers in the hybrid material works as a mech. backbone and an elec. highway, ensuring that a suitable elastic buffer space accommodates the anisotropic expansion of phosphorene layers along the y and z axial directions for stable cycling operation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp8p8kp9p5tl7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgOHHqd5bqjkA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsV2ltrrM&amp;md5=23b94375b97b50394801c280ff017cb8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b07931"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nitta, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lei, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, H.-R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gordon, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gresham, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luzinov, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yushin, G.</span></span> <i>ACS Appl.                 10.1007/s11581-017-2339-8</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DWang%252C%2BL.%253B%2BZhou%252C%2BZ.%253B%2BLi%252C%2BJ.%253B%2BHe%252C%2BX.%2BIonics%2B2018%252C%2B24%252C%2B303%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Fjacs.8b04183&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs11581-017-2339-8&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Red phosphorus composite anodes for Li-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Li; Zhou, Zhaohui; Li, Jiangang; He, Xiangming</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Ionics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">24</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">303-308</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">IONIFA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0947-7047</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Thickness changes assocd. with charging and discharging challenge the practical application of electrodes composed of high-capacity materials towards batteries of both high energy d. and long life.  The investigation on the electrode level has found that the red phosphorus composite electrodes experience reversible expansion and shrinkage during lithiation/delithiation, except for the first cycle.  The reversible thickness changes are acceptable for the practical application, and the thickness change is only 4 μm, which is about 7% of the total thickness when the capacity d. of the electrode is 4.5 mAh cm-2. 4.5 mAh cm-2 is the upper limitation of the capacity d. for practical Li-ion batteries.  The capacity of the electrode material is 800 mAh g-1 with coulombic efficiency of 99.7%.  Such high capacity, high coulombic efficiency, and low electrode thickness change show promise of the red phosphorus composite as a practical anode for lithium-ion batteries.  This investigation pushes forward the practical application of red phosphorus composite anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrsKkFPnbRMurVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgOHHqd5bqjkA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvVensb3I&amp;md5=ee6f0a21038a822746b80b4eb4e5b9a7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.04.168"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span> <i>J.                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Utilizing a graphene matrix to overcome the intrinsic limitations of red phosphorus as an anode material in lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yue, Zishuang; Gupta, Tushar; Wang, Fan; Li, Chao; Kumar, Rajesh; Yang, Zhenyu; Koratkar, Nikhil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">127</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">588-595</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Red P (P) shows enormous potential as a high-performance and cost-effective Li-ion anode material.  It alloys with Li forming Li3P, which translates to a theor. capacity of ∼2595 mAh/g (∼7 times better than graphite).  The cost of bulk P is comparable to battery-grade graphite.  However, there are 2 intrinsic limitations that prevent deployment of P, viz., its low elec. cond. and its high vol. change on cycling that leads to pulverization and loss of elec. contact.  Here, we present an approach to concurrently address both limitations.  We used electro-spraying and far-IR redn. (FIR) to fabricate composites of P and reduced graphene oxide (rGO).  The electro-spraying process enables ultra-small P particles (5-10 nm), which suppresses stress-induced pulverization and drastically reduces Li-ion diffusion distances.  The low elec. cond. of P is also not a limitation at such small particle sizes.  FIR establishes C-P bonds that prevent surface migration and agglomeration of P, and enable efficient electron transfer between the rGO matrix and P nanoparticles.  The P/rGO anode delivers outstanding specific capacity (∼1763 mAh/g at c.d. of ∼0.1 A/g), extraordinary high-rate capability (≤40 A/g) and long cycle-life (&gt;1000 cycles with ∼99% coulombic efficiency).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrJD0kT8EmEQLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgOHHqd5bqjkA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvVKltL%252FF&amp;md5=a59da4079fe23ebf71d32380ef175fd8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1149/2.0351807jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tojo, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamaguchi, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Furukawa, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aoyanagi, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Umezaki, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Inada, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sakurai, Y.</span></span> <i>J.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Solid Electrolyte Interphase Growth and Capacity Loss in Silicon Electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Michan, Alison L.; Divitini, Giorgio; Pell, Andrew J.; Leskes, Michal; Ducati, Caterina; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">138</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">25</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7918-7931</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) of the high capacity anode material Si is monitored over multiple electrochem. cycles by 7Li, 19F, and 13C solid-state NMR spectroscopies, with the orgs. dominating the SEI.  Homonuclear correlation expts. are used to identify the org. fragments -OCH2CH2O-, -OCH2CH2-, -OCH2CH3, and -CH2CH3 contained in both oligomeric species and lithium semicarbonates ROCO2Li, RCO2Li.  The SEI growth is correlated with increasing electrode tortuosity by using focused ion beam and SEM.  A two-stage model for lithiation capacity loss is developed: initially, the lithiation capacity steadily decreases, Li+ is irreversibly consumed at a steady rate, and pronounced SEI growth is seen.  Later, below 50% of the initial lithiation capacity, less Si is (de)lithiated resulting in less vol. expansion and contraction; the rate of Li+ being irreversibly consumed declines, and the Si SEI thickness stabilizes.  The decreasing lithiation capacity is primarily attributed to kinetics, the increased electrode tortuosity severely limiting Li+ ion diffusion through the bulk of the electrode.  The resulting changes in the lithiation processes seen in the electrochem. capacity curves are ascribed to non-uniform lithiation, the reaction commencing near the separator/on the surface of the particles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqlHz_42qOjZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghshJve40m8w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XovVWktr4%253D&amp;md5=b5904ac99b906fa7a6054ce2093b1994</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.03.051"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mortazavi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Birbilis, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Medhekar, N.                         cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">2-Methyltetrahydrofuran-lithium hexafluoroarsenate:  a superior electrolyte for the secondary lithium electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Koch, V. R.; Young, J. H.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1979</span>),

                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCIEAS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0036-8075</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable, high-energy-d. Li batteries require an efficient Li electrode.  Earlier work with electrolytes based on propylene carbonate, Me acetate, and THF yielded poor Li electrode cycling efficiencies because of electrolyte redn. by Li.  Solns. of LiAsF6 in 2-methyltetrahydrofuran  [96-47-9] are remarkably stable toward Li, resulting in cycling efficiencies that approach 98%.  The ability of 2-methyltetrahydrofuran to resist redn. by Li is thought to be based on the position of its lowest unfilled MO relative to that of THF.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoLeyia_DVAw7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghshJve40m8w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaE1MXkt1eqsLo%253D&amp;md5=3b2766ca8796309e40506a3c25305173</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1515/zpch-1937-3725"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Brauer, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zintl, E.</span></span> <i>Z.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">International Union of Crystallography</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The structure of Na3P, known from x-ray powder diffraction data, was refined based on single-crystal data. The framework is built of Na+ and P3- ions arranged in two kinds of layers perpendicular to the c axis.                         cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na3As revisited: high-pressure synthesis of single crystals and structure refinement</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hafner, Peter; Range, Klaus-Juergen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Single crystals of Na3As were obtained from the elements by high-pressure-high-temp. reaction in a modified Belt-type app.  The compd. crystallizes hexagonally, space group P63cm, with a 8.7838(8), c 8.9990(12) Å, and Z = 6.                         cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Two-dimensional sideband separation in magic-angle-spinning NMR</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Antzutkin, Oleg N.; Shekar, S. C.; Levitt, Malcolm H.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Magnetic Resonance, Series A</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1995</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">115</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7-19</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMRAE2</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1064-1858</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Academic</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A new method for sepg. isotropic and anisotropic chem.-shift interactions in magic-angle-spinning NMR is presented.  The new method is based upon manipulation of the phases of spinning sidebands using sequences of five π pulses.  A two-dimensional pulse scheme separates the spinning sidebands by order.  The amplitudes of the spinning sidebands may be analyzed to obtain the principal values of the chem.-shift anisotropy.  For sites with many sidebands, it is possible to improve the signal-to-noise ratio considerably by skew projection of the two-dimensional spectrum.  Exptl. demonstrations are presented.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoV5pMzWCUD2LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liq20QDFd9cXg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK2MXntVOmurw%253D&amp;md5=696b21caa94c97c1480cc58d8024bec7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1351/pac200880010059"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Harris, R.
